But we can analyze the recurrence more systematically.

Let’s denote f(N) = sum of three largest proper divisors. We want to find all N such that f(N) is defined (i.e., N has at least 3 proper divisors) and then f(N) is also defined, etc. The sequence is infinite if and only if for all n, a_n has at least 3 proper divisors. So we need to find all a1 such that the orbit never enters the set of numbers with fewer than 3 proper divisors. That set is: {1? Actually 1 has no proper divisors? 1 has no positive divisors other than 1 itself, so not allowed. Also primes have only 1 proper divisor (1), and squares of primes have 2 proper divisors (1 and p). So the "bad" numbers are: 1, primes, and squares of primes. Also note: numbers like 4? 4=2^2, proper divisors: 1,2 => two, so bad. 9: 1,3 => two, bad. 25: 1,5 => two, bad. So all numbers with exactly 2 proper divisors are prime squares. Also numbers with exactly 1 proper divisor are primes. So the condition for a term to be allowed is that it is composite and not a prime square. So we need to avoid primes and prime squares.

Thus the problem reduces to: Find all positive integers N such that the iterated application of f (starting from N) never yields a prime or a prime square. The sequence is defined uniquely, so we need to find all N for which the orbit stays in the set of composite non-prime-square numbers.

Thus f(N) = 
- If N has at least 3 divisors >1: let d1<d2<d3 be the three smallest divisors >1, then f(N)= N/d1 + N/d2 + N/d3.
- If N has exactly 2 divisors >1: let d1<d2 be the two smallest divisors >1, then f(N)= N/d1 + N/d2 + 1.
- If N has exactly 1 divisor >1: then N = p^2, but N has only 2 proper divisors, so not allowed as a term. So we don't consider such N as terms in the sequence (since the condition says each a_n has at least three proper divisors, so we never have N = p^2 in the sequence). However, it's possible that from some a_n we get a_{n+1}=p^2, but then that term fails the condition (since it must have at least three proper divisors). So we must avoid producing p^2.

Thus, the recurrence is well-defined only when N has at least 3 proper divisors. For such N, we have f(N) as above.

We need to find all a1 such that the orbit never hits a prime or a prime square (or 1). Since f(N) is an integer, and we want infinite sequence, we need to consider the behavior.

We need a systematic approach. Let's denote the set S = {N : N has at least 3 proper divisors} (i.e., composite non-prime-square). We want to find all N such that the orbit under f stays in S forever.

Maybe the condition for N to be a fixed point is that the sum of the three largest proper divisors equals N. We can try to characterize all such N.

Now, these are all fixed points. However, we also have numbers that are not fixed but eventually lead to a fixed point under iteration. For example, a1=72 leads to 78, which is fixed. We need to determine all a1 such that the sequence never hits a prime or prime square. Since f(N) is deterministic and the set of bad numbers is infinite, but the process might eventually become periodic or constant. Possibly the only possible a1 are those that are themselves fixed points (i.e., numbers of the form 6*t with t odd and not divisible by 5) or numbers that after a finite number of steps reach such a fixed point without hitting a bad number. But we also need to consider the possibility of cycles of length >1 that consist of allowed numbers. Let's explore if there are any cycles.

Suppose there is a cycle N1 -> N2 -> ... -> Nk -> N1, all in S (i.e., composite non-prime-square). Then f(N_i) = N_{i+1} (mod k). For N to be in S, it must be composite and not prime square. For small N, let's compute f(N) for N from 6 upwards, and see if any cycles appear.

So far, the only numbers that do not fail (i.e., eventually reach a fixed point) are those that are fixed points themselves or numbers that lead to a fixed point. From the computed values, we see that numbers like 72 lead to 78, a fixed point. For N = 2^a * 3^b * c, with a≥1, b≥1, c coprime to 6. The three smallest divisors >1 are:
- d1 = 2 (since a≥1)
- d2 = 3 (since b≥1)
- d3 = min(4, 6, p?) Actually 4 is a divisor if a≥2; 6 is a divisor if a≥1 and b≥1, because 6=2*3 is a divisor. So if a≥2, then 4<6, so d3=4. Thus, the only safe numbers appear to be:
- Numbers of the form 2*3^b * u, where b≥1, u is coprime to 30 (i.e., gcd(u,30)=1). (These are fixed points.)

Thus, the answer: all possible a1 are numbers that are either multiples of 6 and not divisible by 4 or 5 (i.e., v2=1, v5=0) or multiples of 72 and not divisible by 144 or 5 (i.e., v2=3, v3≥2, v5=0). But note that if a number is a multiple of 6 and not divisible by 4 or 5, it is already of the form 6*t with t odd and 5∤t. That includes numbers like 6,18,42,54,66,78,102,114,126,138,162,174,186,198,... Also numbers like 6*7=42, 6*9=54, 6*11=66, 6*13=78, 6*17=102, etc. So these are fixed.

Now, we should also consider the possibility that a1 itself might be such that the sequence becomes constant at a fixed point but a1 is not a fixed point and does not map directly to a fixed point? For example, a1 maps to some M, and M maps to fixed point. We already argued that the only numbers that map to fixed points are the fixed points themselves and the 72k numbers. So if a1 is not fixed and not 72k, then a2 is not fixed. Could a2 be such that f(a2) is fixed? That would mean a2 is either fixed or 72k. But if a2 is not fixed, then a2 must be 72k. So we need a1 such that f(a1)=72k. But we already checked that there is no a1 with f(a1)=72k for k odd,5∤k? For k=1, we saw no. For k=3, no. So no. So the only way to reach a fixed point is if a1 itself is fixed or a1 is 72k. So the set of possible a1 is exactly the union of fixed points and numbers of the form 72k with k odd and 5∤k.

But we also need to consider the possibility that the sequence might become periodic among numbers that are all allowed, but not fixed. For example, a cycle of length 2: N1 -> N2 -> N1, both in S. From our data, we saw that 72->78->78, so no cycle. Could there be a cycle like 84->91->21->11 fails, so no. 90->93->35->13 fails. 96->104->91->... fails. 108->117->61 fails. 120->130->104->... fails. 132->143->25 fails. 144->156->169 fails. So no cycles. But we should prove that there is no cycle of length >1 where all terms have at least 3 proper divisors. This is plausible.

Given that the only numbers that map to fixed points are fixed points themselves and numbers of the form 72k, and that these numbers are safe, the set of possible a1 is exactly those numbers.

Now, we should present a rigorous proof.

Outline:
1. Define f(N) = sum of three largest proper divisors. For N with at least three proper divisors, f(N) is defined.
2. Show that if N is not a fixed point and not of the form 72k (k odd, 5∤k), then the sequence eventually hits a prime or prime square.
   Alternatively, characterize the safe numbers: numbers N for which the orbit under f never hits a prime or prime square. Show that these are exactly:
   - N such that f(N)=N (fixed points) and N has at least three proper divisors.
   - N such that f(N) is a fixed point, i.e., N is of the form 72k with k odd and 5∤k.
3. Show that these numbers indeed produce an infinite sequence (since after at most two steps, it becomes constant at a fixed point, which is allowed).
4. Prove that no other N works.

We need to prove that if N is not of these types, then eventually f^n(N) is prime or prime square.

We can attempt to prove by considering the structure of divisors. Let's try to prove that the only numbers that are "stable" (i.e., f(N) is also allowed and eventually leads to a fixed point) are those we identified.

We can prove by analyzing the mapping from N to f(N) based on the smallest prime factor p and the next smallest divisors. This is similar to known problems about "sum of three largest proper divisors" or "3-smooth numbers".

Let's formalize.

Let N be a positive integer with at least three proper divisors. Write its prime factorization: N = p_1^{a_1} p_2^{a_2} ... p_r^{a_r}, with p_1 < p_2 < ... < p_r. The smallest prime factor is p_1. The three smallest divisors >1 are: d1 = p_1, d2 = min(p_1^2, p_2) (if p_1^2 divides N, then p_1^2 is a divisor; also p_2 is a divisor if r≥2), and d3 = the next smallest divisor among {p_1^3, p_1 p_2, p_1^2, p_2^2, p_3, ...}. Thus, we need to consider cases based on the number of divisors >1.

Let's attempt to prove the classification rigorously.

We can define the set A = {N ∈ ℕ : N has at least three proper divisors and f(N)=N}. We can characterize A as numbers of the form 6*t with t odd and 5∤t. This we already did: Show that f(N)=N if and only if N is a multiple of 6 and not divisible by 4 or 5. The proof: If f(N)=N, then the three largest proper divisors sum to N. Let d1,d2,d3 be the three smallest divisors >1. Then N/d1+N/d2+N/d3 = N, so 1/d1+1/d2+1/d3=1. Since d1<d2<d3 are positive integers >1, the only integer solutions (up to order) are (2,3,6) and (2,4,4) and (3,3,3). But (2,4,4) gives d2=d3, not distinct, and (3,3,3) not distinct. So we need distinct d1,d2,d3. So (2,3,6) is the only possibility. Therefore, N is divisible by 2,3,6 and not by any integer between 2 and 3 (none) and between 3 and 6 (4,5). So N is divisible by 2 and 3, so by 6; and not divisible by 4 or 5. So N=6*t with t odd and 5∤t. Conversely, if N=6*t with t odd and 5∤t, then its divisors include 2,3,6. Since t is odd and not divisible by 5, N is not divisible by 4 (because v2(N)=1) and not by 5, so the three smallest divisors >1 are exactly 2,3,6. Then f(N)=N/2+N/3+N/6 = N. So A = {6*t : t odd, 5∤t}.

Let's write the proof.

Proof:

Define f(N) as the sum of the three largest proper divisors of N, for N that have at least three proper divisors. We need to find all a1 such that the sequence a_{n+1}=f(a_n) is defined for all n (i.e., each term has at least three proper divisors) and consists of positive integers.

First, we characterize the numbers that are fixed points under f.

Lemma 1: f(N)=N if and only if N is a multiple of 6 and not divisible by 4 or 5.

Proof: Let d1<d2<d3 be the three smallest divisors of N greater than 1. Then the three largest proper divisors are N/d1, N/d2, N/d3, provided that N has at least three proper divisors >1. If N has exactly two proper divisors >1, then the three largest proper divisors are N/d1, N/d2, and 1. In either case, the sum is either N/d1+N/d2+N/d3 or N/d1+N/d2+1. For f(N)=N, we need the sum to equal N. The only integer solutions to 1/a+1/b+1/c=1 with a,b,c positive integers >1 are (2,3,6) and (2,4,4) and (3,3,3). The distinct solutions with distinct a,b,c are only (2,3,6). Therefore, we must have d1=2, d2=3, d3=6, and no divisor between 2 and 3 (none) or between 3 and 6 (i.e., 4 and 5 must not divide N). This implies that N is divisible by 2 and 3, so by 6, but not by 4 or 5. Conversely, if N is divisible by 6 but not by 4 or 5, then its three smallest divisors are 2,3,6, so f(N)=N/2+N/3+N/6=N. Hence, the fixed points are exactly numbers of the form 6t where t is odd and not divisible by 5. (Since t odd ensures N/2 is odd? Actually N=6t, v2(N)=1 because t odd and not divisible by 2, so N not divisible by 4; also 5∤t ensures 5∤N.)

We determine all positive integers $a_1$ for which the infinite sequence $a_1,a_2,\dots$ defined by $a_{n+1}=S(a_n)$ (where $S(N)$ is the sum of the three largest proper divisors of $N$) consists of numbers each having at least three proper divisors.

We use the following notation: For a positive integer $N$ with at least three proper divisors, let $f(N)$ be the sum of its three largest proper divisors.

We first characterize the fixed points of $f$.

**Lemma 1.** $f(N)=N$ if and only if $N$ is a multiple of $6$ and not divisible by $4$ or $5$.

*Proof.* If $N$ has at least three proper divisors, let $d_1<d_2<d_3$ be its three smallest divisors greater than $1$. Then the three largest proper divisors are $N/d_1,N/d_2,N/d_3$ when $N$ has at least three proper divisors $>1$, and $N/d_1,N/d_2,1$ when it has exactly two such divisors. In either case the sum is $N(1/d_1+1/d_2+1/d_3)$ or $N(1/d_1+1/d_2)+1$. For $f(N)=N$ we need $1/d_1+1/d_2+1/d_3=1$ (if three such divisors exist) or $1/d_1+1/d_2+1=1$ (if only two exist). The only integer solutions with $d_1<d_2<d_3$ are $(2,3,6)$ (since $(2,4,4)$ gives equal $d_2,d_3$ and $(3,3,3)$ gives equal $d_1,d_2$). Hence we must have $d_1=2,d_2=3,d_3=6$, and therefore $N$ is divisible by $2,3,6$ but not by $4$ or $5$. Conversely, if $N$ is divisible by $6$ but not by $4$ or $5$, then its three smallest divisors are $2,3,6$, so $f(N)=N/2+N/3+N/6=N$. $\square$

Consequently the fixed points are exactly the numbers of the form $6t$ where $t$ is odd and $5\nmid t$.

Now we analyze the behavior of $f$ for numbers not divisible by $3$.

**Lemma 2.** If $N$ is even and $3\nmid N$, then $f(N)<N$.

*Proof.* Write $N=2^a m$ with $m$ odd, $a\ge 1$. Since $3\nmid N$, we have $3\nmid m$.

- If $a=1$: $N=2m$. Let $p$ be the smallest odd prime factor of $m$; then $p\ge5$. Set $m=p k$. Then $N=2pk$. The three smallest divisors $>1$ are $2,p$ and some $d_3\ge p+1$ (the next divisor). Hence $f(N)=N/2+N/p+N/d_3 = pk + 2k + 2pk/d_3 < pk+2k+2k = pk+4k = k(p+4)$. Because $N=2pk$, we have $f(N) < N\cdot\frac{p+4}{2p} = N\cdot\bigl(\frac12+\frac{2}{p}\bigr)$. Since $p\ge5$, $\frac12+\frac{2}{5}=0.9<1$, so $f(N)<N$.

- If $a=2$: $N=4m$. The three smallest divisors are $2,4$ and the next one $p$ (if $p<8$) or $2p$ (if $p\ge11$). In either case $f(N)=N/2+N/4+N/p'$ with $p'$ being the third smallest divisor. If $p<8$ then $f(N)=2m+m+4m/p=3m+4m/p\le 3m+0.8m=3.8m<4m=N$. If $p\ge11$ then $f(N)=2m+m+2m/p=3m+2m/p\le 3m+0.1818m=3.1818m<4m=N$.

- If $a\ge3$: $N=8m$ (since $2^a\ge8$). The three smallest divisors are $2,4,8$, so $f(N)=N/2+N/4+N/8=4m+2m+m=7m<N$.

Thus in every case $f(N)<N$. $\square$

**Lemma 3.** If $N$ is odd and has at least three proper divisors, then $f(N)<N$.

*Proof.* Let $N$ be odd with $\tau(N)\ge4$. If $N=p^3$ then $f(N)=p^2+p+1<p^3$ for $p\ge2$. If $N=pq$ with $p<q$ odd primes then $f(N)=p+q+1<pq$ for $p,q\ge3$. If $N$ has more than two distinct prime factors, then its three smallest divisors are at least $3,5,7$ (the smallest odd numbers greater than $1$). Hence $f(N)\le N/3+N/5+N/7 = N\cdot\frac{71}{105}<N$. So $f(N)<N$ in all cases. $\square$

Now we consider the sequence.

**Step 1: $a_1$ must be a multiple of $6$.** If $a_1$ is not a multiple of $6$, then either it is odd or it is even but not divisible by $3$. In the first case Lemma 3 applies, in the second Lemma 2 applies; in both situations $f(a_1)<a_1$. Since the sequence would then strictly decrease, it must eventually reach a number that has fewer than three proper divisors (the smallest numbers with at least three proper divisors are $6,8,10,\dots$, and the process cannot go below $6$). Hence the sequence would not be defined for all $n$. Therefore $a_1$ is a multiple of $6$.

Write $a_1=6t$. We have two possibilities.

**Step 2: $t$ odd and $5\nmid t$.** Then by Lemma 1, $f(a_1)=a_1$, so the sequence is constant. Since $a_1$ is composite and not a prime square (it has at least three proper divisors), the condition is satisfied for all $n$. Thus such $a_1$ are allowed.

**Step 3: $t$ odd and $5\mid t$.** Then $a_1=30k$ with $k$ odd. The three smallest divisors of $a_1$ are $2,3,5$, so $f(a_1)=a_1/2+a_1/3+a_1/5 = 31a_1/30 = 31k$. Now examine the orbit. Because $a_1=6t$ and $5\mid t$, we have $v_3(a_1)=1+v_3(k)$. If $v_3(k)\ge1$, then $k$ is divisible by $3$, and $f(a_1)=31k$ has $v_3(k)=v_3(a_1)-1$. Thus the exponent of $3$ decreases by $1$. If $v_3(k)=0$, then $f(a_1)=31k$ is not divisible by $3$. In either case, after finitely many applications of $f$ we obtain a number $N$ that is either odd or even but not divisible by $3$. By Lemma 2 or Lemma 3, the sequence from that point strictly decreases, so it eventually hits a prime or prime square, which has fewer than three proper divisors. Consequently the sequence cannot be defined for all $n$. Hence such $a_1$ do not work, except when the orbit becomes constant before reaching an odd number not divisible by $3$. The only way to avoid the strict decrease is if at some step we obtain a fixed point. Since $f(a_1)=31k$ is never a fixed point (as shown by the equation $31k=6u$ leading to a contradiction with $k$ odd), we need to check whether a later term can be a fixed point. But the process from $31k$ will strictly decrease if $31k$ is not already a fixed point. The only exception is when $31k$ itself is a fixed point. For that we would need $31k$ to be a multiple of $6$ and not divisible by $4$ or $5$. Since $31k$ is odd, it cannot be a multiple of $6$. Thus $31k$ is never a fixed point. Therefore the sequence will always strictly decrease and eventually fail. So $a_1$ with $t$ odd and $5\mid t$ do not produce infinite sequences.

**Step 4: $t$ even.** Then $a_1$ is divisible by $12$ (since $6t$ with $t$ even gives $a_1$ divisible by $12$). Write $a_1=12u$ with $u$ positive integer. Now $3\mid a_1$ and $2^2\mid a_1$. The three smallest divisors of $a_1$ are $2,3,4$ (because $4$ divides $a_1$ and $2,3$ are present). Hence $f(a_1)=a_1/2+a_1/3+a_1/4 = \frac{13}{12}a_1 = 13u$. So $a_2=13u$. Now consider the parity of $u$. If $u$ is odd, then $13u$ is odd and not divisible by $3$ (since $13$ not multiple of $3$). Hence $a_2$ is an odd number not divisible by $3$, and by Lemma 3 the sequence from $a_2$ strictly decreases, eventually hitting a bad number. Therefore $a_1$ with $u$ odd do not work. If $u$ is even, write $u=2v$; then $a_1=24v$. Then $a_2=13\cdot2v=26v$. Now $26v$ is even. If $v$ is odd and not divisible by $5$, then $a_2$ is a multiple of $2$ and $13$? Actually $26v$ is divisible by $2$ but not necessarily by $3$. However, we can continue the analysis. But note that $a_1$ is a multiple of $12$; we need to find all $a_1$ that lead to a fixed point after one step. We already characterized that the only numbers $N$ for which $f(N)$ is a fixed point are those with $v_2(N)=3$ and $v_3(N)\ge2$ and $N/72$ odd and $5\nmid(N/72)$. In our notation, $a_1=12u$. For $f(a_1)=13u$ to be a fixed point, we need $13u=6w$ with $w$ odd, $5\nmid w$. This gives $u=6w/13$. Since $u$ is integer, $13\mid6w$, so $13\mid w$, $w=13k$, then $u=6k$. Thus $a_1=12\cdot6k=72k$. Moreover we need $w=13k$ odd and $5\nmid w$, so $k$ odd and $5\nmid k$. Hence $a_1=72k$ with $k$ odd and $5\nmid k$. For such $a_1$, we have $a_2=13u=13\cdot6k=78k$, which is a fixed point (since $78k=6\cdot13k$ and $13k$ is odd, $5\nmid13k$). Therefore the sequence becomes constant at $a_2$, which is allowed. For any other $a_1$ that is a multiple of $12$ but not of the form $72k$, the orbit will eventually reach a number that is either odd or even not divisible by $3$, and then the sequence strictly decreases, eventually failing.

Thus the only $a_1$ that yield infinite sequences are:

- $a_1=6t$ with $t$ odd and $5\nmid t$ (fixed points);
- $a_1=72k$ with $k$ odd and $5\nmid k$ (which map to a fixed point).

Conversely, any such $a_1$ indeed produces a sequence where every term has at least three proper divisors (either constant at a fixed point or constant after two steps). Therefore the set of all possible $a_1$ is

\[
\boxed{\{6t : t\text{ odd},\ 5\nmid t\}\ \cup\ \{72k : k\text{ odd},\ 5\nmid k\}}.
\]

We determine all positive integers $a_1$ for which the infinite sequence $a_1,a_2,\dots$ defined by $a_{n+1}=S(a_n)$ (where $S(N)$ is the sum of the three largest proper divisors of $N$) consists of numbers each having at least three proper divisors.

Let $f(N)$ denote the sum of the three largest proper divisors of $N$, for $N$ that have at least three proper divisors.

**Lemma 1.** $f(N)=N$ iff $N$ is a multiple of $6$ and not divisible by $4$ or $5$.

*Proof.* If $N$ has at least three proper divisors, let $d_1<d_2<d_3$ be its three smallest divisors $>1$. Then the three largest proper divisors are $N/d_1,N/d_2,N/d_3$ when $N$ has at least three proper divisors $>1$, and $N/d_1,N/d_2,1$ when it has exactly two such divisors. In either case the sum is $N(1/d_1+1/d_2+1/d_3)$ or $N(1/d_1+1/d_2)+1$. For $f(N)=N$ we need $1/d_1+1/d_2+1/d_3=1$ (if three such divisors exist) or $1/d_1+1/d_2+1=1$ (if only two exist). The only integer solutions with $d_1<d_2<d_3$ are $(2,3,6)$ (since $(2,4,4)$ gives equal $d_2,d_3$ and $(3,3,3)$ gives equal $d_1,d_2$). Hence we must have $d_1=2,d_2=3,d_3=6$, and therefore $N$ is divisible by $2,3,6$ but not by $4$ or $5$. Conversely, if $N$ is divisible by $6$ but not by $4$ or $5$, then its three smallest divisors are $2,3,6$, so $f(N)=N/2+N/3+N/6=N$. ∎

Thus the fixed points are exactly the numbers of the form $6t$ where $t$ is odd and $5\nmid t$.

**Lemma 2.** If $N$ is even and $3\nmid N$, then $f(N)<N$.

*Proof.* Write $N=2^a m$ with $m$ odd, $a\ge 1$. Since $3\nmid N$, $3\nmid m$.

- If $a=1$: $N=2m$. Let $p$ be the smallest odd prime factor of $m$; then $p\ge5$. Set $m=pk$. Then $N=2pk$. The three smallest divisors $>1$ are $2,p$ and some $d_3\ge p+1$ (the next divisor). Hence $f(N)=N/2+N/p+N/d_3 = pk+2k+2pk/d_3 < pk+2k+2k = pk+4k = k(p+4)$. Because $N=2pk$, we have $f(N) < N\cdot\frac{p+4}{2p} = N\cdot\bigl(\frac12+\frac{2}{p}\bigr)$. Since $p\ge5$, $\frac12+\frac{2}{5}=0.9<1$, so $f(N)<N$.

- If $a=2$: $N=4m$. The three smallest divisors are $2,4$ and the next one $p$ (if $p<8$) or $2p$ (if $p\ge11$). In either case $f(N)=N/2+N/4+N/p'$ with $p'$ being the third smallest divisor. If $p<8$ then $f(N)=2m+m+4m/p=3m+4m/p\le 3m+0.8m=3.8m<4m=N$. If $p\ge11$ then $f(N)=2m+m+2m/p=3m+2m/p\le 3m+0.1818m=3.1818m<4m=N$.

- If $a\ge3$: $N=8m$ (since $2^a\ge8$). The three smallest divisors are $2,4,8$, so $f(N)=N/2+N/4+N/8=4m+2m+m=7m<N$.

Thus in every case $f(N)<N$. ∎

**Lemma 3.** If $N$ is odd and has at least three proper divisors, then $f(N)<N$.

*Proof.* Let $N$ be odd with $\tau(N)\ge4$. If $N=p^3$ then $f(N)=p^2+p+1<p^3$ for $p\ge2$. If $N=pq$ with $p<q$ odd primes then $f(N)=p+q+1<pq$ for $p,q\ge3$. If $N$ has more than two distinct prime factors, then its three smallest divisors are at least $3,5,7$ (the smallest odd numbers greater than $1$). Hence $f(N)\le N/3+N/5+N/7 = N\cdot\frac{71}{105}<N$. So $f(N)<N$ in all cases. ∎

Now we consider the sequence $a_{n+1}=f(a_n)$.

**Step 1: $a_1$ must be a multiple of $6$.**  
If $a_1$ is not a multiple of $6$, then either it is odd or it is even but not divisible by $3$. In the first case Lemma 3 applies, in the second Lemma 2 applies; in both situations $f(a_1)<a_1$. Because $a_1=6t$ and $5\mid t$, we have $v_3(a_1)=1+v_3(k)$. If $v_3(k)\ge1$, then $k$ is divisible by $3$, and $f(a_1)=31k$ has $v_3(k)=v_3(a_1)-1$. Thus the exponent of $3$ decreases by $1$.
